Salah — Prayer
intermediate
8 min
Traveler Following Resident Imam and Vice Versa
Rulings on iqtida between traveler and resident in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a traveler follows a resident imam and explain why the traveler must complete four rak'ahs.

Core Idea
A traveler following a resident imam must complete four rak'ahs because following changes his obligation.

When a traveler prays behind a resident imam, a key question arises: does he pray two rak'ahs (as a traveler) or four (following the imam)? The Hanafi school rules that the traveler must complete four rak'ahs, regardless of when he joins. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, exceptions, and the opposite case of a resident following a traveler.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Determine whether a traveler is following a resident imam (the case) or a resident following a traveler (the opposite).
The ruling differs for each scenario.
2
Understand the obligation change
The traveler's obligation changes from two to four rak'ahs due to the validity of following (iqtida). This is a key Hanafi principle.
The change occurs only if the following occurs within the prayer time.
3
Apply the ruling to the traveler-follows-resident case
Within the time, the traveler completes four rak'ahs. After the time, he does not follow. Also, if he invalidates his prayer, he returns to two rak'ahs.
There is a distinction between following with fard intention vs. nafl intention.
4
Consider the opposite case
When a resident follows a traveler, it is valid both within and after the time. The resident completes the remaining rak'ahs after the traveler's salam.
The resident does not recite in the remaining rak'ahs in the most sound view.
5
Note the Maliki disagreement
Imam Malik holds that if the traveler catches only the last pair of rak'ahs, he should not exceed his traveler's pair.
This difference has practical implications for the follower's prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن اقتدى مسافر بمقيم في الوقت صح وأتم وبعده لا، وبعكسه صح فيهما. وأتم: أي المسافر صلاته مع الإمام مطلقاً سواء أدركه في الشفع الأول أو الثاني؛ لأنه تغير فرضه إلى أربع لصحة الاقتداء، وإن أفسد الفرض يصلي ركعتين؛ لأن لزوم الأربع للمتابعة وقد زالت بخلاف ما لو اقتدى به بنية النفل ثم أفسد، حيث يلزمه الأربع بالشروع رمز الحقائق: ٩٥/١] وعند مالك: إذا أدركه في الشفع الأخير لا يجاوز شفعه. (عيني، مسكين وبعده لا [ولو اقتدي به بعد خروج الوقت. (ط)] لأن فرضه لا يتغير بعد الوقت لانقضاء السبب، كما لا يتغير بنية الإقامة، فيكون اقتداء المفترض بالمتنفل في حق القعدة الأولى؛ إذ هي فريضة في حق المسافر وفي حق القراءة؛ لأن قراءته في الأخريين نفل رمز الحقائق: ٩٦/١] (فتح) وبعكسه: هو ما لو اقتدي مقيم بمسافر. (ط) صح فيهما: إذا اتفق الفرضان، لأنه يكون اقتداء متنفل مفترض في حق القعدة، فإن سلم المسافر يتم المقتدي ولا يقرأ فيما بقي؛ لأنه كاللاحق، وقيل: يقرأ كالمسبوق. [رمز الحقائق: ٩٦/١] ويستجب للإمام أن يقول: "أتموا صلاتكم فإنا قوم سفر"؛ لأنه عليها قال هذا حين صلى بمكة وهو مسافر. (فتح) فيهما: أي في الوقت وبعده وإذا سلم المسافر يتم المقيم بلا قراءة في الأصح ويستحب للإمام أن يقول لهم: أتموا صلاتكم فإنا قوم سفر. (ط)
And if a traveler follows a resident in prayer within the time, it is valid and he completes (the prayer), and after the time, no; and its opposite is valid in both. 'And he completes': meaning the traveler prays his prayer with the imam absolutely, whether he catches him in the first pair or the second, because his obligation has changed to four due to the validity of following; and if he invalidates the obligation, he prays two rak'ahs because the necessity of four is for following and it has ceased, unlike if he followed him with the intention of supererogatory then invalidated, where the four are incumbent by commencement. According to Malik: if he catches him in the last pair, he should not exceed his pair. 'And after it no': even if he followed after the exit of the time, because his obligation does not change after the time due to the termination of the cause, just as it does not change by intention of residence, so it becomes the following of a muftarid by a mutanawwil regarding the first sitting, for it is obligatory in the right of the traveler, and regarding the recitation because his recitation in the last two is supererogatory. 'And its opposite': that is when a resident follows a traveler. 'Valid in both': when the two obligations agree, because it is following of a mutanawwil by a muftarid regarding the sitting; if the traveler says salam, the follower completes and does not recite in what remains because he is like a lahiq, and it is said he recites like a masbuq. And it is mustahabb for the imam to say: 'Complete your prayer, for we are a traveling group' because the Prophet (peace be upon him) said that when he prayed in Mecca while traveling. 'In both': meaning within the time and after it; and when the traveler says salam, the resident completes without recitation in the most sound view; and it is mustahabb for the imam to say to them: Complete your prayer, for we are a traveling group.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage explains the rulings when a traveler prays behind a resident imam or vice versa. If a traveler follows a resident imam within the prayer time, the traveler must complete four rak'ahs (perform 'atamm') because by following, his obligation becomes four. If he catches only the last part of the prayer, he still completes four. The same rule applies even if he invalidates his prayer? No, if he invalidates, he must pray two rak'ahs because the obligation of four was only for following. If he intended supererogatory and then invalidated, he must complete four. After the time has expired, a traveler cannot follow a resident because his obligation is already fixed. If a resident follows a traveler, it is valid in both cases (within and after time) because the two obligations agree. The resident completes the remaining rak'ahs after the traveler says salam, without recitation in the most sound view. The imam is recommended to inform the congregation that he is traveling so they can complete.

Key Terms
الاقتداء (al-iqtida'): Following an imam in congregational prayer.
الشفع (al-shaf'): A pair of rak'ahs in prayer.
أتم (atamma): Completed the prayer fully (i.e., performed the full four rak'ahs).
اللاحق (al-lahiq): One who joins the prayer late but catches the imam before the salam.
المسبوق (al-masbuq): One who misses one or more rak'ahs and then completes them after the imam's salam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a traveler follows a resident imam within the prayer time, how many rak'ahs does the traveler pray?
A) Two rak'ahs.
B) Four rak'ahs.
C) The traveler can choose.
D) One rak'ah.

Key Takeaway
A traveler following a resident imam must complete four rak'ahs because the validity of following changes his obligation from two to four.

The Three Types of Homeland and Their Abrogation
Defines three homeland types and their abrogation rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the three types of homeland, state which are valid in the Hanafi school, and explain how each is abrogated.

Core Idea
Your original homeland is only lost by settling elsewhere permanently; travel or temporary residence does not break it.

The Hanafi school classifies a Muslim's affiliation to a place into three categories: original homeland, residence homeland, and sojourn homeland. This classification affects the traveler's prayer obligation, specifically whether one prays full or shortened prayers. This lesson unpacks each type and the rules for how they are invalidated, as stated in Kanz al-Daqa'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Homelands
List the three types of homelands defined in the text: original (asli), residence (iqama), and sojourn (sukna). Note the defining conditions for each.
Original homeland is birthplace or permanent settlement; residence homeland is intention to stay 15+ days; sojourn homeland is intention to stay less than 15 days.
2
Understand Abrogation of Original Homeland
The text states that the original homeland is only nullified by another original homeland. The proof is the Prophet's migration from Mecca to Medina. When he and the Companions returned to Mecca for pilgrimage, they prayed as travelers.
This shows that traveling or temporary residence does not break the original homeland.
3
Apply Abrogation Rules to Each Homeland
For each homeland, determine what nullifies it. Original: only by another original. Residence: by original, another residence, or travel. Sojourn: by all of the above. Note that the verifying scholars do not consider sojourn homeland valid.
The principle: a thing is invalidated by its like or superior, not by what is inferior.
4
Recognize the Preferred Hanafi View
The text concludes that the reliable view (al-sahih) is to reject the sojourn homeland as a valid category. Only two homelands are recognized: original and residence.
This is mufta bihi and supported by later Hanafi authorities like al-Fath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويبطل الوطن الأصلي بمثله. الوطن الأصلي هو مولد الإنسان أو البلدة التي تأهل فيها رمز الحقائق ٩٦/١٢] والأوطان ثلاثة: أصلي ووطن إقامة ووطن السكنى وهو ما يكون بنية الإقامة أقل من خمسة عشر يوماً، فالأول ينتقض بمثله؛ لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم والمهاجرين من أصحابه كانوا من أهل مكة ثم لما هاجروا واستقروا بالمدينة انتقض وطنهم بمكة حتى كانوا إذا أتوا مكة يصلون صلاة المسافرين ولا يبطل بالآخرين ولا بالسفر، والثاني يبطل بالأول وبالثاني وبالسفر، والثالث يبطل بالكل وبالسفر. ولم يعتبر المحققون وطن السكنى وهو الصحيح. [رمز الحقائق: ٩٦/١] (فتح) لا السفر: أي بإنشائه؛ لأنه دونه ولا يبطل بوطن الإقامة أيضاً. (ط، ع) ووطن الإقامة: الموضع الذي ينوي المسافر أن يقم فيه خمسة عشر يوماً فصاعداً. (ع) والسفر: أي بإنشاء السفر؛ لأنه ضده. (ط) والأصلي: أي بالوطن الأصلي، والأصل أن الشيء يبطل بمثله وبما فوقه لا بما دونه. (ط)
And the original homeland is nullified by its like. The original homeland is one's birthplace or the town in which one has settled. And the homelands are three: original, homeland of residence, and homeland of sojourn, which is with the intention of residence less than fifteen days. The first is abrogated by its like because the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the emigrants among his companions were from the people of Mecca, then when they migrated and settled in Medina, their homeland in Mecca was abrogated so that when they came to Mecca they prayed the prayer of travelers; and it is not invalidated by the others nor by travel. The second is invalidated by the first, by the second, and by travel. The third is invalidated by all and by travel. And the verifying scholars do not consider the homeland of sojourn, and it is the correct view. 'Not by travel': meaning by initiating it, because it is inferior and is not invalidated by the homeland of residence either. And the homeland of residence is the place where the traveler intends to stay for fifteen days or more. 'And by travel': meaning by initiating travel, because it is its opposite. 'And by the original': meaning by the original homeland; the principle is that a thing is invalidated by its like and by what is above it, not by what is below it.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of homelands and explains how each is invalidated. The original homeland (watan asli) is the place of birth or where one has settled. It is only nullified by another original homeland, as the Prophet and his companions when they migrated from Mecca to Medina became travelers when returning to Mecca. The homeland of residence (watan iqama) is where a traveler intends to stay fifteen days or more; it is nullified by the original homeland, by another residence homeland, or by travel. The homeland of sojourn (watan sukna) is where one intends to stay less than fifteen days; it is nullified by any of the others or by travel. However, the reliable scholars do not consider watan sukna as a valid homeland, and that is the correct view. The passage also clarifies that initiating travel does not nullify the original homeland, and that the original homeland is only nullified by its equal or superior, not by inferior categories.

Key Terms
الوطن الأصلي (al-watan al-asli): One's birthplace or the town where one has permanently settled.
وطن الإقامة (watan al-iqama): A place where a traveler intends to stay fifteen days or more.
وطن السكنى (watan al-sukna): A place where one intends to stay less than fifteen days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of homeland is nullified by initiating travel?
A) Original homeland.
B) Homeland of residence.
C) Homeland of sojourn.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, only the original homeland and the homeland of residence are recognized; the sojourn homeland is not considered valid.

The Make-up Prayer Mirrors the Performance
Ruling on make-up prayers for traveler and resident

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the number of rak'ahs for make-up prayers for a traveler and a resident, and explain the Hanafi principle that governs it.

Core Idea
Qada mirrors ada: traveler makes up 2, resident makes up 4.

When a Muslim misses an obligatory prayer, they must make it up later. But how many rak'ahs should they pray? The Hanafi school answers with a clear rule: the make-up prayer mirrors the original performance at the time the prayer was missed. This lesson unpacks that ruling and the underlying principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is making up a missed obligatory prayer (qada). The ruling is that it must be performed in the same number of rak'ahs as when it was originally due.
The number depends on the person's status at the time of missing, not at the time of making up.
2
Recognize the governing principle
The principle is 'al-qada' yahki al-ada' (القضاء يحكي الأداء) – the make-up mirrors the performance. This is a key Hanafi maxim.
This principle is also applied in other areas of fiqh.
3
Apply to traveler and resident
A traveler who misses a 4-rak'ah prayer (Dhuhr, Asr, Isha) must make up 2 rak'ahs. A resident must make up 4 rak'ahs. The number for Fajr and Maghrib remains unchanged (2 and 3 respectively).
4
Note the cross-madhhab disagreement
Imam al-Shafi'i held that there is no concession (rukhsah) in make-up prayers, so a traveler would make up 4 rak'ahs even if missed while traveling.
The Hanafi position is based on the principle that qada mirrors the original obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفائتة السفر والحضر تقضي ركعتين وأربعا. أي وتقضى ركعتين وأربعا فيه لف ونشر فركعتين يرجع إلى فائتة السفر، وأربعاً يرجع إلى فائتة الحضر؛ وذلك لأن القضاء يحكي الأداء. [رمز الحقائق: ٩٦/١] وقال الشافعي: لا رخصة في فائتة السفر أيضاً. (مسكين) فيه: أي في كل واحد من السفر والإقامة وكذا في الحيض والطهر والبلوغ والإسلام. (ع)
And the missed prayer of travel and residence are made up as two rak'ahs and four. i.e., they are made up as two and four; in it is lubb wa nashr: two refers to missed of travel, four refers to missed of residence; because the make-up mirrors the performance. And al-Shafi'i said: There is no concession in the missed prayer of travel either. 'In it': meaning in each of travel and residence, and similarly in menstruation and purity, puberty, and Islam.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This ruling states that when making up missed prayers, the traveler must make up two rak'ahs per prayer (qasr) and the resident four. The principle is that qada' (make-up) mirrors ada' (performance). Al-Shafi'i disagreed, holding that there is no concession for the traveler in make-up prayers, but the Hanafi position is clear. The commentary also notes that the same principle applies to cases of menstruation, puberty, etc.

Key Terms
فائتة (fai'tah): A missed obligatory prayer that is made up later.
القضاء يحكي الأداء (al-qada' yahki al-ada'): The make-up prayer mirrors the original performance in number and quality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a traveler misses Dhuhr while traveling, how many rak'ahs does he make up?
A) 4 rak'ahs.
B) 2 rak'ahs.
C) 1 rak'ah.
D) 3 rak'ahs.

Key Takeaway
The make-up prayer mirrors the original performance in number; traveler makes up 2, resident makes up 4.

End of Time Determines Traveler's Prayer Rak'ahs
End of time determines traveler's prayer obligation duration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi criterion of 'end of time' to determine whether a traveler prays 2 or 4 rak'ahs, and explain the proofs and ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
The traveler's prayer is determined by his status at the end of the time, not the beginning.

In the Hanafi school, the moment that fixes the number of rak'ahs for a traveler is the end of the prayer time. This seemingly technical point has important practical implications: a resident who travels late in the time, or a child who reaches puberty just before time ends, will have their obligation shaped by that final moment. In this lesson, we unpack the ruling, its rational proof, and the disagreements of Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The passage states: 'And the criterion in it is the end of the time.' This means that to determine whether a person prays the traveler's (2 rak'ahs) or resident's (4 rak'ahs) prayer, look at his status at the end of the prayer time.
The ruling applies when the prayer was not performed earlier in the time.
2
Understand the rational proof (dalil aqli)
The proof is: the obligation of prayer is established only after the time fully elapses without performance. The minimum required to 'catch' the time is the opening takbir (tahrimah). Therefore, the criterion is the status at the last moment capable of tahrimah.
This is a rational argument based on causality.
3
Apply the proof to analogous cases
The text gives two examples: a boy who reaches puberty or a disbeliever who accepts Islam at the end of the time (with enough time for tahrimah) become obliged to pray; their number of rak'ahs depends on their status at that moment.
These cases follow the same principle.
4
Contrast with Zufar's position
Zufar holds that the criterion is the remaining time sufficient to complete the full prayer. So if a resident travels at the end and enough time remains to pray 2 rak'ahs, he shortens; if less remains, he prays full 4.
Zufar's view is within the Hanafi school but is not the relied-upon position.
5
Contrast with al-Shafi'i's position
Al-Shafi'i considers the beginning of the time as the criterion. So if a person is a traveler at the start, he prays 2 even if he becomes a resident later; if a resident at start, he prays 4 even if he travels later.
This is a major inter-madhhab difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمعتبر فيه آخر الوقت. [فإن كان آخر الوقت مسافراً وجب عليه ركعتان وإن كان مقيماً فأربع. (ط)] لأنه المعتبر في السببية حال عدم الأداء قبله، وذا بقدر التحريمة، فلو بلغ صبي أو أسلم كافر في آخر الوقت بقدرها وجبت عليهم، وقال زفر يعتبر قدر ما يتمكن من أداء الصلاة، حتى لو سافر المقيم في آخر الوقت وبقي منه قدر ما يتمكن أن يصلى فيه ركعتين قصر، وإن بقي أقل منه أتم عنده وقال الشافعي يعتبر أول الوقت. (فتح، مسكين وغيره)
And the criterion in it is the end of the time. [For if at the end of the time he is a traveler, then two rak'ahs are obligatory upon him; and if he is a resident, then four.] Because it is the criterion in causality when there was no performance before it; and that is to the extent of the tahrimah. So if a boy reaches puberty or a disbeliever accepts Islam at the end of the time to that extent, it becomes obligatory upon them. And Zufar said: He considers the extent with which he is able to perform the prayer, so that if the resident travels at the end of the time and there remains of it enough to pray two rak'ahs, he shortens; and if less remains, he completes according to him. And al-Shafi'i said: He considers the beginning of the time.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage clarifies that the determining factor for the traveler's prayer is the state at the end of the prayer time, not the beginning or middle. If at the end of the time a person is a traveler, he owes two rak'ahs; if a resident, four. This is because the obligation is established when the time ends and the prayer has not been performed. The criterion is applied at the moment just enough for the opening takbir (tahrimah). Zufar disagreed, considering the remaining time enough to complete the prayer. Al-Shafi'i considered the beginning of the time. The Hanafi position is that the end of time is the criterion.

Key Terms
آخر الوقت (akhir al-waqt): The end of the prayer time; the last moment when the prayer can be performed.
التحريمة (al-tahrimah): The opening takbir of the prayer; the minimal act that 'catches' the prayer time.
السببية (al-sababiyyah): Causality; the relationship between the cause (end of time) and the obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person is a resident at the beginning of prayer time but travels and becomes a traveler at the end of the time, how many rak'ahs does he owe?
A) Four rak'ahs.
B) Two rak'ahs.
C) Depends on intention.
D) It is invalid.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the traveler's prayer rak'ahs are determined by the person's status at the very end of the prayer time, not at the beginning or by the ability to complete.

The Sinner Traveler and Travel Concessions
Dispute on sinner traveler's concessions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on travel concessions for a sinner, identify the Shafi'i counter-argument, and evaluate the Hanafi response based on textual generality.

Core Idea
Travel itself is not a sin; the concession attaches to travel, not to the traveler's intention.

This passage addresses a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: does a person traveling for a sinful purpose still qualify for the travel concessions of shortening prayers and breaking fast? The Hanafi school holds that such a traveler does, based on the unqualified language of the Quran and Hadith. The Shafi'i school argues that since the concession is a relief, it should not apply to one who deserves severity due to sin. Let us examine the evidence on both sides.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: a sinner traveler (e.g., highway robber) is entitled to both qasr and iftar, just like any other traveler.
The Arabic phrase 'العاصي كغيره' encapsulates this.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that al-Shafi'i disagrees: no concession for the sinner because sin warrants severity.
The opponent's logic: relief should not attach to that which demands severity.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the Shafi'i argument: the very purpose of the concession is relief; sin contradicts that purpose.
They consider the character of the traveler, not just the act of travel.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi rebuttal relies on two points: (a) the Quranic and Hadith texts are absolute ('on a journey' without qualification); (b) travel itself is not a sin—the sin is separate.
The analogy to sale during Friday call (valid despite sin) reinforces the point.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: in Hanafi, the sinner prays 2 rak'ahs and may break fast; in Shafi'i, he must pray 4 rak'ahs and fast.
This difference directly affects the worshiper's obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعاصي كغيره. أي من سافر سفر المعصية كقطاع الطريق واللصوص يترخصون بالقصر والإفطار كما يترخص من سافر سفر الطاعة كالغزاة والحجاج، وهذا عندنا، وقال الشافعي: لا رخصة للعاصي؛ لأن الرخصة ثبت تخفيفاً فلا يتعلق بما يوجب التغليظ، وهو المعصية، ولنا: إطلاق النصوص؛ كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنْ كَانَ مِنْكُمْ مَرِيضاً أَوْ عَلَى سَفَرٍ ﴾ (البقرة:١٨٤) وقوله عليه: "صلاة المسافر ركعتان" إلى غير ذلك؛ ولأن نفس السفر ليس بمعصية، وإنما المعصية ما يكون بعده أو معه، والرخصة تتعلق بالسفر لا بالمعصية؛ لأن المعصية المجاورة لا تنفي الأحكام كالبيع عند النداء. تبيين الحقائق: ٥٢١/١] (مستخلص، فتح)
And the sinner is like others. i.e., the one who travels a sinful journey like highway robbers and thieves; they are allowed concessions of shortening and breaking fast just as those who travel a journey of obedience like warriors and pilgrims; this is our position. And al-Shafi'i said: There is no concession for the sinner because the concession is established as a relief, so it is not attached to that which necessitates severity, namely sin. And our evidence: the generality of texts like His saying: 'So whoever among you is ill or on a journey' (2:184) and his saying (peace be upon him): 'The prayer of the traveler is two rak'ahs' and so on; and because the travel itself is not a sin; rather the sin is what occurs after or with it, and the concession is attached to the travel, not the sin, because an accompanying sin does not negate rulings, just like sale at the time of the call to prayer.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute. The Hanafi position is that a person who travels for a sinful purpose, such as a highway robber or thief, still qualifies for the concessions of travel: shortening prayers and breaking fast in Ramadan. Al-Shafi'i disagreed, holding that since the purpose is sinful, the concession is not deserved. The Hanafi proof is based on the absolute texts of Quran and Hadith that do not restrict travel concession to obedient travel. The key point is that the travel itself is not a sin; the sin is separate. Just as a sale at the time of the Jumu'ah call is valid despite the accompanying sin, so too the travel concession remains.

Key Terms
العاصي (al-'asi): A person who travels for a sinful purpose, e.g., highway robbery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, can a highway robber shorten his prayer while traveling?
A) Yes, because travel itself is not a sin.
B) No, because his sin negates the concession.
C) Only if he repents.
D) Yes, but he must pray full prayer as a punishment.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that travel concessions apply to all travelers regardless of purpose, because the concession is attached to the act of travel, not to the traveler's intention.

Following in Travel: Who Is a Follower?
Definition of follower in travel with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify who qualifies as a follower (taba') in travel and apply the conditions of dependency and knowledge that affect the follower's prayer rulings.

Core Idea
A follower in travel adopts the principal's status only if dependent and aware of the principal's intention.

In Hanafi fiqh, certain individuals are considered 'followers' (taba') in travel rulings—they do not form their own travel intention but adopt the status of the person they depend on. This lesson clarifies who these followers are and the conditions under which their prayer is valid or binding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories of followers
List all persons mentioned as followers: woman (wife), slave, soldier, hired worker, student, captive. Understand that the list is illustrative, not exhaustive.
The core principle: the follower is 'subordinate' to a principal (husband, master, commander, employer, teacher, captor).
2
Examine the dependency condition for soldiers
A soldier is a follower only if he receives provisions from the commander. If he is merely attached to the commander in another way (e.g., nominal affiliation), he is not a follower.
This condition reflects the general rule that the follower must be dependent on the principal for livelihood or command.
3
Apply the knowledge condition
The follower is bound by the principal's travel status only if he knows the principal's intention to travel or stay. If the follower does not know, he may validly pray as a traveler, and his prayer is not required to be repeated if the principal later changes status.
The rationale: imposing a ruling without knowledge causes hardship (haraj).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسفر من الأصل دون التبع أي المرأة والعبد والجندي. فإنها تبع لزوجها فإنه تبع لأميره. والجندي: يعني من كان في طاعة الغير يصير مسافراً بسفره ومقيماً بإقامته تحقيقاً للتبعية كالمرأة تكون تبعاً للزوج إذا أوفاها مهرها المعجل، والعبد تبع للسيد، والجيش تبع للأمير، وكذلك الأجير تبع للمستأجر، والتلميذ تبع للأستاذ، والأسير لمن أسره، وإنما يكون الجندي تبعاً إذا كان يرتزق من الأمير، فإذا كان مضافاً إليه لا يكون تبعاً. رمز الحقائق ٩٧/١] ثم هذا إذا علم التبع نية إقامة الأصل، أما إذا لم يعلم فلا حتى لو صلى التبع صلاة المسافرين قبل علمه بنية الأصل فإن صلاته جائزة، ولا يجب عليه إعادتها؛ لأن في اللزوم بدون العلم ضرراً في حقه وحرجاً. (مستخلص، عيني)
And travel is from the origin, not from the follower, i.e., the woman, the slave, and the soldier. For she is a follower of her husband, and he (the soldier) is a follower of his commander. And the soldier: meaning the one who is in obedience to another; he becomes a traveler by his travel and a resident by his residence, effectuating the following, like the woman who is a follower of her husband if he gives her her prompt dower, and the slave is a follower of the master, and the army is a follower of the commander, and similarly the hired worker is a follower of the employer, and the student is a follower of the teacher, and the captive is a follower of the one who captured him. The soldier is a follower only if he receives provisions from the commander; if he is attached to him (in a different way), he is not a follower. Then this applies when the follower knows the intention of residence of the principal; but if he does not know, then not, even if the follower prays the traveler's prayer before knowing the principal's intention, his prayer is permissible and he is not required to repeat it because requiring in absence of knowledge would cause harm and hardship for him.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of tab'iyyah (following) in travel rulings. Certain individuals are considered followers in travel: a woman with her husband, a slave with his master, a soldier with his commander. They adopt the travel status of the principal. For example, if the husband is traveling, the wife is also considered a traveler even if she does not personally have the intention. The same applies to a soldier who depends on his commander. The commentary lists several categories: hired worker, student, captive. However, the soldier is only a follower if he receives his livelihood from the commander; otherwise, he is not a follower. Additionally, if the follower does not know the principal's intention, he is not bound by it, and if he has already prayed as a traveler, his prayer is valid and does not need to be repeated.

Key Terms
التبع (al-taba'): A person who follows another (principal) in travel rulings, such as wife, slave, soldier.
التابعية (al-tabi'iyyah): The state of being a follower in travel.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does a soldier become a follower in travel according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) Always, because he is in the army.
B) Only if he receives provisions from the commander.
C) Only if he personally intends travel.
D) Never, he must have his own intention.

Key Takeaway
A follower in travel is bound by the principal's status only when dependent on the principal (e.g., financially) and aware of the principal's intention; otherwise, the follower's own prayer as a traveler is valid and need not be repeated.

Definitions of Watan and Nullification of Travel
Defines watan al-iqamah, watan asli, and rules of nullification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the three types of watan in Hanafi fiqh and explain what nullifies travel status.

Core Idea
Travel status is nullified only by establishing residence (15+ days) or returning home — not by merely starting a new journey.

This lesson unpacks a dense passage from the chapter on traveler's prayer. It clarifies a common confusion: simply beginning a new journey does not cancel your existing travel ruling; only returning to your original home or intending to stay fifteen days or more in a place will make you a resident. The passage also defines the key terms 'watan al-iqamah' and 'watan asli' and states the underlying legal principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the negative statements
The passage begins with four negations: travel is not nullified by its own inception, nor by watan al-iqamah, nor by the inception of travel, nor by the original home. Parse which negations are actually affirmative rules.
The four negations are: لا السفر، ووطن الإقامة بمثله، والسفر، والأصلي. The commentary clarifies that 'السفر' and 'الأصلي' are actually affirmations of nullification.
2
Extract the definition of watan al-iqamah
The passage defines watan al-iqamah as a place where a traveler intends to stay fifteen days or more. This intention, once formed, makes the traveler a muqim (resident) who must pray full prayers.
The fifteen-day minimum is a Hanafi position; other schools differ.
3
Understand nullification by watan asli
Returning to one's original homeland (watan asli) nullifies travel status. This is an example of nullification by 'like' because returning to a permanent home is the opposite of traveling.
The principle: a thing is nullified by its like or by what is above it.
4
Apply the general principle
The passage states: 'the principle is that a thing is invalidated by its like and by what is above it, not by what is below it.' Travel status (a thing) is nullified by establishing residence (its opposite, 'above it') or returning home (its like), but not by merely intending another journey (below it).
This principle is used elsewhere in fiqh, e.g., for purity states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب صلاة المسافر لا السفر، ووطن الإقامة بمثله، والسفر، والأصلي. لا السفر: أي بإنشائه؛ لأنه دونه ولا يبطل بوطن الإقامة أيضاً. (ط، ع) ووطن الإقامة: الموضع الذي ينوي المسافر أن يقم فيه خمسة عشر يوماً فصاعداً. (ع) والسفر: أي بإنشاء السفر؛ لأنه ضده. (ط) والأصلي: أي بالوطن الأصلي، والأصل أن الشيء يبطل بمثله وبما فوقه لا بما دونه. (ط)
Chapter on the Prayer of the Traveler: [Travel's being made null] is not by travel itself, nor by the place of residence, nor by travel, nor by the original [home]. 'Not by travel' means: by initiating it; because it is inferior to it, and it is not invalidated by the place of residence either. (T, A) And the place of residence: the place where the traveler intends to stay fifteen days or more. (A) And 'by travel' means: by initiating travel; because it is its opposite. (T) And 'the original' means: by the original home; and the principle is that a thing is invalidated by its like and by what is above it, not by what is below it. (T)
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a definition-ruling from the chapter on traveler's prayer. It begins by stating that travel is not nullified by its own inception (i.e., the act of beginning a journey does not cancel the legal status of being a traveler), nor by entering a place of residence (watan al-iqamah), nor by the inception of travel itself (which seems contradictory but refers to the opposite), nor by returning to the original homeland (watan asli). The commentary clarifies each term: 'لا السفر' means that the legal ruling of travel is not nullified by merely intending or beginning a journey, because the intended journey is 'below' (dūn) the actual travel ruling; also, it is not invalidated by a place of residence (where one intends to stay 15+ days). 'ووطن الإقامة' is defined as a place where a traveler intends to reside for fifteen days or more, at which point he ceases to be a traveler and must pray fully. 'والسفر' (with 'al-') refers to the initiation of travel itself, which is the opposite of stay and thus nullifies the previous stay. 'والأصلي' refers to the original homeland; returning there nullifies travel. The general principle stated is that a thing (here, the ruling of travel) is nullified by its like (e.g., returning to the original homeland nullifies travel) and by what is above it (e.g., becoming a resident in a new homeland nullifies travel), but not by what is below it (e.g., merely intending travel does not nullify the current travel ruling). This defines the three types of 'watan' in Hanafi fiqh: watan asli (original home), watan al-iqamah (temporary residence of 15+ days), and watan al-safar (travel). The student should note that watan al-safar is a very temporary status, easily nullified.

Key Terms
وطن الإقامة (watan al-iqāmah): A place where a traveler intends to stay fifteen days or more; upon such intention, he becomes a resident (muqīm) and must pray full prayers.
وطن أصلي (watan aṣlī): The place where a person was born or has taken as a permanent home with the intention of settling; it nullifies travel status upon return.
لا السفر (lā al-safar): The act of initiating travel does not nullify the prior travel status; i.e., simply starting a journey does not cancel the existing travel ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many days must a traveler intend to stay in a place to become a resident (muqīm) and stop shortening prayers?
A) 10 days
B) 15 days
C) 20 days
D) 30 days

Key Takeaway
Travel status is nullified only by watan asli (original home) or watan al-iqamah (15-day stay), never by merely starting a new journey.

Makeup Prayers: The Principle of Qada Mirroring Ada
Ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle 'al-qada' yahki al-ada' to determine the number of rak'ahs for makeup prayers when the state of travel or residence changes, and contrast the Shafi'i ruling.

Core Idea
A missed prayer is made up as it was obligated, not as when performed.

When you miss a prayer while traveling and later make it up as a resident, how many rak'ahs do you pray? The Hanafi answer relies on a key principle: the makeup (qada) mirrors the original performance (ada). This lesson unpacks this ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explaining the rhetorical device used and the Shafi'i disagreement, so you can confidently apply the rule in changing circumstances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rhetorical structure
The matn says 'two and four' — a 'laff wa nashr' (wrapping and unfolding). Determine which number refers to which type of missed prayer.
Two refers to travel, four to residence.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
A missed travel prayer is made up as 2 rak'ahs; a missed residence prayer as 4 rak'ahs — regardless of the current state when making up.
The key is the state at the time the prayer was missed.
3
Explain the underlying principle
The principle 'al-qada' yahki al-ada' means the substitute (qada) replicates the original (ada) in conditions and number.
This is a foundational Hanafi legal maxim.
4
Present the Shafi'i counter-position
Imam al-Shafi'i held that the rukhsah (concession) of shortening does not carry over to makeup; the current state determines the number.
For him, if a traveler misses a prayer and makes it up as a resident, he prays 4.
5
Analyze the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: if a traveler misses Dhuhr and returns home, Hanafi prays 2, Shafi'i prays 4. Vice versa: if resident misses then travels, Hanafi prays 4, Shafi'i prays 2.
This difference affects every missed prayer with a change in state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأربعاً فيه لف ونشر فركعتين يرجع إلى فائتة السفر، وأربعاً يرجع إلى فائتة الحضر؛ وذلك لأن القضاء يحكي الأداء. [رمز الحقائق: ٩٦/١] وقال الشافعي: لا رخصة في فائتة السفر أيضاً. (مسكين)
And 'four' in it is a wrapping and unfolding: 'two' refers to the missed prayer of travel, and 'four' refers to the missed prayer of residence; and that is because the makeup mirrors the performance. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/96] And al-Shafi'i said: There is no concession (rukhsah) in the missed prayer of travel either. (Miskin)
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author is explaining the matn phrase '... تقضي ركعتين وأربعا', noting that the text uses a 'wrapping and unfolding' (laff wa nashr) rhetorical device: 'two' refers to the makeup of a missed travel prayer (which was originally two rak'ahs), and 'four' refers to the makeup of a missed residence prayer (which was originally four rak'ahs). The legal principle is that the makeup (qada) of a prayer mirrors the original performance (ada) in terms of number of rak'ahs. Thus, if a traveler misses a prayer while traveling, he makes it up as two rak'ahs even if he is now a resident; conversely, if a resident misses a prayer, he makes it up as four even if he is currently traveling. The passage then notes that Imam al-Shafi'i disagreed, holding that there is no concession (rukhsah) in makeup prayers, meaning that if a traveler missed a prayer while traveling but is now a resident, he must make it up as four. The Hanafi position is that the makeup is based on the time of obligation (the state when the prayer was missed), not the time of performance. This is based on the hadith 'Pray as you see me praying' and the principle that qada' is a substitute for the original.

Key Terms
القضاء يحكي الأداء (al-qaḍā' yaḥkī al-adā'): The principle that makeup (qada) of an act of worship mirrors the original performance (ada) in terms of conditions and number.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person misses a Dhuhr prayer while traveling (he was a traveler at the time). Later he returns home and makes it up. How many rak'ahs should he pray?
A) 2 rak'ahs
B) 4 rak'ahs
C) 3 rak'ahs
D) It depends on his intention

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule for makeup prayers is based on the state at the time of the missed prayer, not the time of makeup, following the principle that qada mirrors ada.

The End of Time Determines the Traveler's Prayer
Determines which ruling applies based on the end of prayer time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rule that the end of prayer time determines whether a traveler shortens the prayer, and evaluate the opposing views of Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Traveler's prayer is ruled by his state at the end, not the start, of the time.

When a prayer is delayed until the last moment, which state matters: the state at the beginning of the time or at the end? The Hanafi school answers with a clear rule: the end of the time is decisive. This lesson unpacks that principle and the scholarly disagreement around it, helping you see how a single rule can shape the obligation of daily prayers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its criterion
Determine what act is being ruled (traveler's prayer) and what criterion the Hanafi school uses (end of the prayer time).
The same principle applies to menstruation, purity, puberty, and embracing Islam.
2
Understand the Hanafi reasoning (sababiyyah)
Read the author's justification: the end of time is the causal moment when the prayer is actually performed if delayed. This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on the concept of causation.
The minimum time considered is just the opening takbirah (tahrimah).
3
Examine Zufar's objection
Zufar holds that if a resident travels at the end of time, he must have enough time remaining to perform the entire prayer (not just takbirah) for the shortening to apply.
This is an intra-Hanafi difference.
4
Examine al-Shafi'i's objection
Al-Shafi'i considers the beginning of the prayer time as the criterion. If one is a traveler at the start, he shortens; if resident, he prays fully, regardless of later changes.
This is a cross-madhhab difference.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Apply each view to a concrete scenario: a resident who travels during Dhuhr and has enough time for two rak'ahs at the end. Hanafi says pray 2; Shafi'i says pray 4. Zufar says depends on whether enough time for the full prayer remains.
The Hanafi position is the mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيه: أي في كل واحد من السفر والإقامة وكذا في الحيض والطهر والبلوغ والإسلام. (ع) آخر الوقت: [فإن كان آخر الوقت مسافراً وجب عليه ركعتان وإن كان مقيماً فأربع. (ط)] لأنه المعتبر في السببية حال عدم الأداء، قبله، وذا بقدر التحريمة، فلو بلغ صبي أو أسلم كافر في آخر الوقت بقدرها وجبت عليهم، وقال زفر يعتبر قدر ما يتمكن من أداء الصلاة، حتى لو سافر المقيم في آخر الوقت وبقي منه قدر ما يتمكن أن يصلى فيه ركعتين قصر، وإن بقي أقل منه أتم عنده وقال الشافعي يعتبر أول الوقت. (فتح، مسكين وغيره)
In it: i.e., in each one of travel and residence, and likewise in menstruation, purity, puberty, and Islam. (A) End of the time: [If the end of the time finds him a traveler, two rak'ahs are obligatory upon him; if a resident, four. (T)] Because it is the consideration in causation at the time of non-performance, before it, and that is to the extent of the takbirah al-tahrimah. If a boy reaches puberty or a disbeliever accepts Islam at the end of the time to that extent, it becomes obligatory upon them. Zufar said: Consideration is the amount [of time] sufficient to perform the prayer, so if a resident travels at the end of the time and there remains from it sufficient to pray two rak'ahs, he shortens; if less remains, he prays fully according to him. And al-Shafi'i said: Consideration is the beginning of the time. (Fath, Miskin, and others)
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof concerning the criterion for whether a prayer is shortened or performed fully. It begins by noting that the same principle applies not only to travel and residence but also to other states like menstruation, purity, puberty, and embracing Islam. The core ruling is that the end of the prayer time is what determines the obligation: if a person is a traveler at the end of the time for a prayer, he must pray two rak'ahs; if a resident, four. The reason given is that the time of the end is what establishes the causal link (sababiyyah) when the prayer has not been performed earlier. The extent of time considered is just enough for the opening takbirah (tahrimah). Thus, if a boy reaches puberty or a disbeliever accepts Islam at the end of the time with enough time for the takbirah, the prayer becomes obligatory upon them. The passage then presents two opposing views: Imam Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) said that the consideration is the amount of time sufficient to perform the entire prayer. So if a resident travels at the end of the time and enough time remains to pray two rak'ahs, he should shorten; if less remains, he must pray four. Imam al-Shafi'i said that the beginning of the time is what matters: if one was a traveler at the start, he shortens even if he becomes a resident later. The Hanafi position (the author's) is that the end of the time is the criterion, and this is the relied-upon view in the madhhab.

Key Terms
آخر الوقت (ākhir al-waqt): The end of the prayer time; the state of the person at this moment determines whether the prayer is shortened or done fully if the prayer was delayed.
بقدر التحريمة (biqadr al-taḥrīmah): The extent of time equal to saying the opening takbirah (Allahu Akbar) and starting the prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A resident starts the time of Dhuhr as a resident, then travels before the end of the time. At the very end of the time, he is a traveler. How many rak'ahs must he pray for Dhuhr according to the Hanafi school?
A) 2 rak'ahs
B) 4 rak'ahs
C) It depends on how much time remains
D) He must make it up later

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school determines the traveler's prayer based on his state at the end of the prayer time, not the beginning.

intermediate

The Disobedient Traveler’s Right to Travel Concessions
Dispute on the disobedient traveler's eligibility for rukhsah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the disobedient traveler’s travel concessions and explain the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i objection.

Core Idea
A sinful journey still qualifies for travel concessions; the journey itself is not the sin.

This lesson addresses a classical dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: does a person who travels for a sinful purpose—like highway robbery—still get the concessions of shortening prayers and breaking the fast? The Hanafi school affirms this based on the generality of the Quranic and Prophetic texts, while the Shafi'i school argues that concessions are a lightening that cannot attach to disobedience. We will analyze the proofs and understand the Hanafi reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the dispute
State the Hanafi position: the disobedient traveler (al-‘āṣī) gets all travel concessions. Then note that Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that concessions cannot apply to acts of disobedience.
The dispute is inter-madhhab, not intra-Hanafi.
2
Examine the Shafi'i proof and its logic
Shafi'i reasoning: The concession (rukhsah) is a lightening (takhfīf) and cannot attach to something that warrants severity (tashdīd), i.e., disobedience. The act of disobedience is incompatible with divine leniency.
This is a qiyās argument based on the nature of rukhsah.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof from textual evidence
The Hanafi school relies on the general application (ʿumūm) of Quranic verses and hadiths, such as 'if you are on a journey' (Qur'an 2:184) and 'The prayer of the traveler is two rakʿahs.' These texts do not discriminate by purpose.
The Hanafi usul principle: al-amm yufīdu al-ḥukm; general texts apply to all instances unless excluded by a specific evidence.
4
Understand the Hanafi counter-argument
The Hanafi response: The journey itself is not a sin; the sin is the accompanying act (e.g., theft). The concession attaches to the journey (safar), not to the sin. An adjacent sin does not negate rulings, just as a sale conducted during the Friday call is sinful but still valid (the contract is not void).
This uses the principle that sin and validity are separate; validity is determined by legal capacity (ahliyyah) and conditions.
5
Note the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilāf: According to Hanafis, a highway robber can shorten his Zuhr prayer to 2 rakʿahs and break his fast during Ramadan while on his journey. Shafi'is deny these concessions.
The difference is outcome: the robber’s prayer is valid in both schools, but only Hanafis consider it shortened; Shafi'is require full prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعاصي: [بالسفر مثل قاطع الطريق والسارق ونحوه. (ط،ع)] أي من سافر سفر المعصية كقطاع الطريق واللصوص يترخصون بالقصر والإفطار كما يترخص من سافر سفر الطاعة كالغزاة والحجاج، وهذا عندنا، وقال الشافعي الله: لا رخصة للعاصي؛ لأن الرخصة ثبت تخفيفاً فلا يتعلق بما يوجب التغليظ، وهو المعصية، ولنا: إطلاق النصوص؛ كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنْ كَانَ مِنْكُمْ مَرِيضاً أَوْ عَلَى سَفَرٍ ﴾ (البقرة:١٨٤) وقوله عليه: "صلاة المسافر ركعتان" إلى غير ذلك؛ ولأن نفس السفر ليس بمعصية، وإنما المعصية ما يكون بعده أو معه، والرخصة تتعلق بالسفر لا بالمعصية؛ لأن المعصية المجاورة لا تنفي الأحكام كالبيع عند النداء. تبيين الحقائق: ٥٢١/١] (مستخلص، فتح) كغيره: من المطيعين في الترخص برخص المسافرين. (ط، ع) والجندي: يعني من كان في طاعة الغير يصير مسافراً بسفره ومقيماً بإقامته تحقيقاً للتبعية كالمرأة تكون تبعاً للزوج إذا أوفاها مهرها المعجل، والعبد تبع للسيد، والجيش تبع للأمير، وكذلك الأجير تبع للمستأجر، والتلميذ تبع للأستاذ، والأسير لمن أسره، وإنما يكون الجندي تبعاً إذا كان يرتزق من الأمير، ر، فإذا كان مضافا إليه لا يكون تبعاً. رمز الحقائق ٩٧/١] ثم هذا إذا علم التبع نية إقامة الأصل، أما إذا لم يعلم فلا حتى لو صلى التبع صلاة المسافرين قبل علمه بنية الأصل فإن صلاته جائزة، ولا يجب عليه إعادتها؛ لأن في اللزوم بدون العلم ضرراً في حقه وحرجاً. (مستخلص، عيني)
And the disobedient: [like the highway robber and thief and the like. (T, A)] i.e., one who travels for disobedience, like highway robbers and thieves, they may avail themselves of concessions of shortening and breaking the fast just as one who travels for obedience, like warriors and pilgrims; and this is with us. And al-Shafi'i said: There is no concession for the disobedient, because the concession is established as a lightening, so it does not attach to that which necessitates severity, i.e., disobedience. And our evidence: the general application of texts, such as His saying, 'So whoever among you is ill or on a journey' (al-Baqarah 184) and his saying, 'The prayer of the traveler is two rak'ahs,' etc. And also because the journey itself is not disobedience; disobedience is what comes after or with it, and the concession is attached to the journey, not to the disobedience, because an adjacent disobedience does not negate the rulings, like a sale at the time of the call [to Friday prayer]. [Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: 1/521] (Mukhtasar, Fath) 'Like others': i.e., the obedient, in availing themselves of the concessions of travelers. (T, A) And the soldier: meaning one who is under the obedience of another becomes a traveler by his travel and a resident by his residence, in realization of subordination, like the woman being subordinate to the husband if he pays her prompt dower, and the slave subordinate to the master, and the army subordinate to the commander, and likewise the employee subordinate to the employer, the student subordinate to the teacher, and the captive to the one who captured him. And the soldier is only a subordinate if he receives sustenance from the commander; but if he is attached to him, he is not a subordinate. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/97] Then this is if the subordinate knows the intention of residence of the principal; if he does not know, then no, even if the subordinate prayed the traveler's prayer before knowing the principal's intention, his prayer is valid, and he is not obligated to repeat it, because requiring it without knowledge would cause him harm and hardship. (Mukhtasar, 'Ayni)
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage covers two distinct but connected rulings. First, it addresses the disobedient traveler (al-'asi), meaning one who travels for a sinful purpose such as highway robbery or theft. The Hanafi position is that such a person still qualifies for the travel concessions—shortening prayers and breaking the fast in Ramadan—just like a traveler on an obedient journey (e.g., pilgrimage or jihad). The text provides the proof: the general verses and hadiths about the traveler do not exclude the disobedient. The response to al-Shafi'i is that the journey itself is not sinful; the sin is in the act committed during the journey or accompanying it. The concession is attached to the act of traveling, not to the moral character of the traveler, analogous to a sale being valid despite being conducted during the Friday sermon (though sinful). Second, the passage discusses the follower (al-tabi')—the person whose travel status is determined by the person he follows. Examples: a wife follows her husband, a slave follows his master, a soldier follows his commander, an employee follows his employer, a student follows his teacher, a captive follows his captor. The ruling is that such a follower becomes a traveler or resident based on the state of the principal. However, this only applies if the follower is aware of the principal's intention. If he does not know, and prays as a traveler, his prayer is valid and need not be repeated, because obligating him to repeat would cause harm and hardship (haraj).

Key Terms
العاصي (al-‘āṣī): One who travels specifically for a sinful purpose (e.g., highway robbery, theft).
رخصة (rukhṣah): A legal concession that relaxes a general ruling due to a valid excuse (e.g., travel, illness).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the status of the traveler who travels to commit highway robbery regarding travel concessions?
A) He cannot shorten prayers because he is a sinner.
B) He may shorten prayers and break the fast just like any other traveler.
C) He may shorten prayers but must still fast.
D) He must pray the full prayer but may break the fast.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school grants travel concessions to all travelers regardless of the purpose of the journey, because the texts are general and the journey itself is not sinful.

The Follower’s Travel Ruling: When Does a Follower Become a Traveler?
Ruling on the follower's travel status based on subordination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a follower (tabi') adopts the travel status of his principal, and explain the ruling when the follower is unaware of the principal's intention.

Core Idea
A follower becomes a traveler by the travel of his principal, but only if he knows of the principal's intention.

This lesson explains the concept of tabi'iyyah (subordination) in travel rulings. Certain individuals like a wife, a slave, a soldier, an employee, a student, and a captive follow the travel status of their respective principals. However, this subordination requires knowledge: if the follower does not know the principal's intention to stay or travel, he prays according to his own status and his prayer is valid. This prevents hardship (haraj).

Guided Path
1
Identify who qualifies as a follower
List the examples given in the text: wife, slave, soldier, employee, student, captive. These are individuals who are under the obedience of another (the principal).
The soldier must receive sustenance from the commander to be considered a follower; otherwise, he is independent.
2
Understand the rule of subordination
The follower becomes a traveler when the principal travels, and a resident when the principal resides. This is called tabi'iyyah; it is a legal construction (hukm wad'i) that links the follower's status to the principal's.
This is not a hukm taklifi; it is a rule of attribution.
3
Note the condition of knowledge
The subordination is effective only if the follower knows the principal's intention regarding travel or residence. If the follower is unaware, his own travel status applies.
Knowledge is a condition (shart) for the ruling to bind.
4
Determine the consequence if unaware
If the follower prays as a traveler (shortening) before learning the principal's intention, his prayer is valid and needs no repetition. This is to avoid harm and hardship (haraj).
This is based on the principle that obligations are not imposed without knowledge.
5
Apply to scenarios
Practice: A wife travels with her husband. The husband intends to stay for 10 days but does not inform her. She prays Zuhr as 2 rak'ahs. Ruling: Her prayer is valid. Later, if she learns, she does not repeat.
The follower is not required to investigate; the default is his own status.

Key Terms
التبع (al-taba‘): A person who follows another (e.g., wife, slave, soldier) and whose travel ruling is determined by the follower's principal's intention.
الأصل (al-aṣl): The principal or leader whose travel status determines the follower's status.
حرج (ḥaraj): Hardship or difficulty; a key legal principle that rulings causing hardship are mitigated.

B) The follower's prayer is based on his own status; if he prayed as a traveler, it is valid.

Key Takeaway
A follower's travel status depends on the principal's intention, but only if the follower knows that intention; otherwise, the follower prays based on his own status and it is valid.

The City Condition for Friday Prayer: Proof and Hanafi Position
Defines the city condition and provides proof and cross-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition that Friday prayer must be performed in a city (misr) with an amir and qadi, and explain the proof from the saying of Ali ibn Abi Talib.

Core Idea
Friday prayer is invalid in a village without a ruler and judge; the saying of Ali proves it.

The Hanafi school requires that Friday prayer be held in a major city (misr) that has executive and judicial authority. This condition is proven by the saying of Ali (ra) that no Friday prayer is valid except in a major city. In this lesson, we explore the definition of misr and the evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
The passage states that one condition for the performance (ada') of Friday prayer is the misr. Understand that this is a shart al-ada', meaning its absence invalidates the prayer.
2
Examine the definition of misr
The text gives multiple definitions; the relied-upon one is that it must have an amir and qadi who enforce hudud. Note the intra-Hanafi differences but focus on the mufta bihi.
3
Locate the proof
The proof is the saying of Ali: 'لا جمعة ولا تشريق إلا في مصر جامع'. Understand that this is a statement of the Sahaba and is used as a proof in Hanafi usul.
4
Understand the cross-madhhab dispute
The Shafi'i school permits Friday in villages based on a hadith of Ibn Abbas. The Hanafi response distinguishes that the place was a fortified city, not a village.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب صلاة الجمعة شرط أدائها المصر، وهو كل موضع له أمير وقاض ينفذ الأحكام ويقيم الحدود، صلاة الجمعة أي المصر أي حده الشرعية باب صلاة الجمعة في بعض النسخ: باب الجمعة بحذف مضاف لكثرة الاستعمال، وفي بعضها: باب صلاة الجمعة وهو اختيار بعض الشراح، والجمعة مشتقة من الاجتماع؛ لاجتماع الناس أو لما جاء من جمع خلق آدم فيها، أو جمعه مع حواء في الأرض وهي بسكون الميم، والقراء يقرءون بضم الميم، وكان اسمها في الجاهلية العروبة، وقيل: أول من سماها جمعة كعب بن لوي ويسمى يوم المزيد؛ لتزايد الخيرات فيه، أو لتزايد الثواب، وقد يطلق عليه العيد أيضاً وهي فرض عين يكفر جاحدها؛ لثبوتها بالدليل القطعي، وهي فرض مستقل أكد من الظهر، وليست بدلاً عنه والمناسبة بين البابين أن في كليهما سقوط شطر الصلاة أن نسبتها إلى الظهر شطر لا أنها تنصيف الظهر بعينه؛ لأنها فرض ابتداء. (فتح) شرط أدائها : اعلم أن لوجوبها شرائط، وهي في المصلى كما سيأتي، ولأدائها شرائط، وهي في غير المصلى، والفرق بين شرائط الوجوب وشرائط الأداء: أن بانتفاء الأول يصح الأداء، وبانتفاء الثاني لا يصح، ونظم بعضهم شروط الأداء والوجوب فقال: حر يعني صحيح بالبلوغ مذكر مقيم وذو عقل لشرط وجوبها ومصر وسلطان ووقت وخطبة وإذن كذا جمع لشرط أدائها والحاصل: أن شرائط الأداء أيضاً ستة: الأول: المصر فلم تجز في قرية على الصحيح، وعن أبي يوسف القرية إن كانت داخل السور فعلى أهلها الجمعة، وإلا فلا وعن محمد كل قرية يسمع أهلها الأذان فعليهم الجمعة، خلافاً للشافعي؛ لحديث ابن عباس لما أنها أقيمت بمسجد عبد القيس بجوائي قرية من قرى البحرين، ولنا: قول علي انه "لا جمعة ولا تشريق" إلى آخره، ولا حجة له في حديث ابن عباس هما؛ لأن جوائي اسم لحصن بالبحرين، وهي مدينة والمدينة تسمى قرية كما ورد في القرآن (مسكين، فتح المصر فلا تجوز في قرية ولا مفازة؛ لقول علي : لا جمعة ولا تشريق ولا صلاة فطر ولا أضحى إلا في مصر جامع. (ط،ع) أمير وقاض: يحرس الناس ويمنع المفسدين ويقوي أحكام الشرع. (ع) ويقيم الحدود: [هذا عند أبي يوسف وهو الصحيح، وفي رواية عنه المصر الجامع كل موضع أهلها كثير بحيث لو اجتمعوا في أكبر مساجدهم لم يسعهم. (عيني)] أي يرجم المحصن الزاني، ويجلد غير المحصن، ويقطع السارق، ويحد القاذف وشارب الخمر، ويحكم بالقود والدية ونحوها، وتفسير المصر بكل موضع له أمير إلخ رواية عن أبي يوسف وهو ظاهر المذهب، وهو الصحيح، وعليه أكثر الفقهاء، وقال أبو حنيفة: المصر كل بلدة فيها سكك، وأسواق ولها رساتيق يعني القرى، ووال ينصف المظلوم من ظالمه وعالم يرجع إليه في الحوادث وهو الأصح وقيل في تفسير المصر أقوال أخر، وأحسن ما قيل فيه: إذا كان أهلها بحال لو اجتمعوا في أكبر مساجدهم لم يسعهم، وهو رواية عن الثاني. (فتح، مستخلص)
Chapter on Friday Prayer: A condition for its performance is the city, which is every place that has an amir and a qadi who execute rulings and enforce the hudud penalties. ... Friday prayer is derived from 'gathering'; it is a fard 'ayn, its denier becomes a disbeliever due to its establishment by decisive proof. It is an independent fard more emphasized than Dhuhr, and not a substitute for it. Conditions of performance: know that its obligation has conditions which are in the one praying, and its performance has conditions which are outside the one praying. The difference: if the former are absent, the performance is valid; if the latter are absent, it is not valid. The conditions of performance are six: the city (misr), [the sultan, time, khutbah, permission, and congregation]. The city: Friday is not valid in a village according to the correct view. From Abu Yusuf: a village if it is within a wall, its inhabitants must pray Friday; otherwise no. From Muhammad: every village that can hear the adhan, its inhabitants must pray Friday. This contradicts al-Shafi'i, based on hadith of Ibn Abbas that Friday was established in a village of Bahrain. Our evidence is the saying of Ali: 'No Friday, no Eid prayer except in a major city.' The hadith of Ibn Abbas is not an evidence because it refers to a fortress which is a city. (Miskin, Fath) The city is not valid in a village or desert. (T, A) An amir and a qadi: to protect people, prevent mischief, and enforce Islamic rulings. (A) And to enforce the hudud: this is from Abu Yusuf and it is the correct view. According to another narration from him, the main city is every place with a large population such that they would not fit in their largest mosque. ('Ayni) The definition of the city as a place with an amir etc. is a narration from Abu Yusuf and it is the apparent madhhab and correct. Abu Hanifa said: The city is every town that has streets, markets, and rural districts (quran) and a ruler who does justice and a scholar to refer to for events. That is the most correct. There are other definitions; the best is that its population is such that they would not fit in their largest mosque, which is a narration from the second. (Fath, Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on Friday prayer defines the key condition for its validity: the prayer must be held in a major city (misr jami'). It begins by noting the linguistic derivation of 'jumu'ah' and its legal status as a fard 'ayn, established by decisive proof such that its denier is considered a disbeliever. Then it distinguishes between conditions of obligation (shurut al-wujub) and conditions of performance (shurut al-ada'), using a poetic summary. The condition under discussion is the first of the performance conditions: the city. The author states the correct view is that Friday is not valid in a village (qaryah). There are three positions: Abu Hanifa's definition requires the city to have streets, markets, rural districts, a just ruler, and a scholar. Abu Yusuf's definition requires an amir and qadi who enforce hudud. Another narration from Abu Yusuf defines it by population size (too many to fit in the largest mosque). The preferred definition is that of Abu Yusuf (with amir and qadi) as it is 'zahir al-madhhab'. The text also discusses the hadith of Ibn Abbas about a village in Bahrain and rebuts it by noting that the place was actually a fortress (hisn) which qualifies as a city. The proof for the condition is the saying of 'Ali ibn Abi Talib: 'لا جمعة ولا تشريق إلا في مصر جامع' (No Friday prayer and no Eid prayer except in a major city). Thus, the Hanafi school requires for Friday prayer a settlement with an executive authority and a judge to maintain order and enforce Islamic criminal law.

Key Terms
المصر (al-miṣr): A major city with an amir (governor) and a qadi (judge) who execute legal judgments and enforce hudud penalties.
شرائط الأداء (shurūṭ al-adā'): Conditions that must be present for the Friday prayer to be valid; if any is missing, the prayer is not valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
According to the Hanafi school, Friday prayer is only valid in a major city (misr) governed by an amir and qadi who enforce Islamic law.

Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf on the Definition of Misr
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the definition of misr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three Hanafi opinions on the definition of misr and state which is mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf's definition of misr as having an amir and qadi is the relied-upon Hanafi position.

The Hanafi school has multiple definitions of what constitutes a city (misr) for Friday prayer. Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad each have distinct views. This lesson dissects these opinions and explains why the definition requiring an amir and qadi is preferred.

Guided Path
1
State the different opinions
List the views of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf (two narrations), and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
2
Identify the preferred view
The text indicates that the definition from Abu Yusuf (first narration) is the apparent madhhab (zahir al-madhhab) and correct.
3
Understand the justification
The preference is based on the criterion of having executive and judicial authority to enforce hudud.
4
Note the tarjih authority
The text says 'wa huwa al-sahih' and 'wa 'alayhi akthar al-fuqaha' - this is the tarjih.

باب صلاة الجمعة شرط أدائها المصر، وهو كل موضع له أمير وقاض ينفذ الأحكام ويقيم الحدود، صلاة الجمعة أي المصر أي حده الشرعية باب صلاة الجمعة في بعض النسخ: باب الجمعة بحذف مضاف لكثرة الاستعمال، وفي بعضها: باب صلاة الجمعة وهو اختيار بعض الشراح، والجمعة مشتقة من الاجتماع؛ لاجتماع الناس أو لما جاء من جمع خلق آدم فيها، أو جمعه مع حواء في الأرض وهي بسكون الميم، والقراء يقرءون بضم الميم، وكان اسمها في الجاهلية العروبة، وقيل: أول من سماها جمعة كعب بن لوي ويسمى يوم المزيد؛ لتزايد الخيرات فيه، أو لتزايد الثواب، وقد يطلق عليه العيد أيضاً وهي فرض عين يكفر جاحدها؛ لثبوتها بالدليل القطعي، وهي فرض مستقل أكد من الظهر، وليست بدلاً عنه والمناسبة بين البابين أن في كليهما سقوط شطر الصلاة أن نسبتها إلى الظهر شطر لا أنها تنصيف الظهر بعينه؛ لأنها فرض ابتداء. (فتح) شرط أدائها : اعلم أن لوجوبها شرائط، وهي في المصلى كما سيأتي، ولأدائها شرائط، وهي في غير المصلى، والفرق بين شرائط الوجوب وشرائط الأداء: أن بانتفاء الأول يصح الأداء، وبانتفاء الثاني لا يصح، ونظم بعضهم شروط الأداء والوجوب فقال: حر يعني صحيح بالبلوغ مذكر مقيم وذو عقل لشرط وجوبها ومصر وسلطان ووقت وخطبة وإذن كذا جمع لشرط أدائها والحاصل: أن شرائط الأداء أيضاً ستة: الأول: المصر فلم تجز في قرية على الصحيح، وعن أبي يوسف القرية إن كانت داخل السور فعلى أهلها الجمعة، وإلا فلا وعن محمد كل قرية يسمع أهلها الأذان فعليهم الجمعة، خلافاً للشافعي؛ لحديث ابن عباس لما أنها أقيمت بمسجد عبد القيس بجوائي قرية من قرى البحرين، ولنا: قول علي انه "لا جمعة ولا تشريق" إلى آخره، ولا حجة له في حديث ابن عباس هما؛ لأن جوائي اسم لحصن بالبحرين، وهي مدينة والمدينة تسمى قرية كما ورد في القرآن (مسكين، فتح المصر فلا تجوز في قرية ولا مفازة؛ لقول علي : لا جمعة ولا تشريق ولا صلاة فطر ولا أضحى إلا في مصر جامع. (ط،ع) أمير وقاض: يحرس الناس ويمنع المفسدين ويقوي أحكام الشرع. (ع) ويقيم الحدود: [هذا عند أبي يوسف وهو الصحيح، وفي رواية عنه المصر الجامع كل موضع أهلها كثير بحيث لو اجتمعوا في أكبر مساجدهم لم يسعهم. (عيني)] أي يرجم المحصن الزاني، ويجلد غير المحصن، ويقطع السارق، ويحد القاذف وشارب الخمر، ويحكم بالقود والدية ونحوها، وتفسير المصر بكل موضع له أمير إلخ رواية عن أبي يوسف وهو ظاهر المذهب، وهو الصحيح، وعليه أكثر الفقهاء، وقال أبو حنيفة: المصر كل بلدة فيها سكك، وأسواق ولها رساتيق يعني القرى، ووال ينصف المظلوم من ظالمه وعالم يرجع إليه في الحوادث وهو الأصح وقيل في تفسير المصر أقوال أخر، وأحسن ما قيل فيه: إذا كان أهلها بحال لو اجتمعوا في أكبر مساجدهم لم يسعهم، وهو رواية عن الثاني. (فتح، مستخلص)
Chapter on Friday Prayer: A condition for its performance is the city, which is every place that has an amir and a qadi who execute rulings and enforce the hudud penalties. ... Friday prayer is derived from 'gathering'; it is a fard 'ayn, its denier becomes a disbeliever due to its establishment by decisive proof. It is an independent fard more emphasized than Dhuhr, and not a substitute for it. Conditions of performance: know that its obligation has conditions which are in the one praying, and its performance has conditions which are outside the one praying. The difference: if the former are absent, the performance is valid; if the latter are absent, it is not valid. The conditions of performance are six: the city (misr), [the sultan, time, khutbah, permission, and congregation]. The city: Friday is not valid in a village according to the correct view. From Abu Yusuf: a village if it is within a wall, its inhabitants must pray Friday; otherwise no. From Muhammad: every village that can hear the adhan, its inhabitants must pray Friday. This contradicts al-Shafi'i, based on hadith of Ibn Abbas that Friday was established in a village of Bahrain. Our evidence is the saying of Ali: 'No Friday, no Eid prayer except in a major city.' The hadith of Ibn Abbas is not an evidence because it refers to a fortress which is a city. (Miskin, Fath) The city is not valid in a village or desert. (T, A) An amir and a qadi: to protect people, prevent mischief, and enforce Islamic rulings. (A) And to enforce the hudud: this is from Abu Yusuf and it is the correct view. According to another narration from him, the main city is every place with a large population such that they would not fit in their largest mosque. ('Ayni) The definition of the city as a place with an amir etc. is a narration from Abu Yusuf and it is the apparent madhhab and correct. Abu Hanifa said: The city is every town that has streets, markets, and rural districts (quran) and a ruler who does justice and a scholar to refer to for events. That is the most correct. There are other definitions; the best is that its population is such that they would not fit in their largest mosque, which is a narration from the second. (Fath, Mustakhlas)

The Ruling of Friday Prayer: Fard 'Ayn and Independent Obligation
Definition and legal classification of Friday prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of Friday prayer (Jumu'ah) as fard 'ayn, explain why denial is kufr, and distinguish it from Zuhr as an independent obligation.

Core Idea
Friday prayer is fard 'ayn, independent of Zuhr, and its denial constitutes kufr due to definitive evidence.

In this lesson, we examine the foundational ruling of Friday prayer (Jumu'ah) as presented in the Hanafi tradition. The passage clarifies not only the legal status but also the etymological and historical background, emphasizing that Jumu'ah is a distinct obligation, not a substitute for Zuhr. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rulings of Jumu'ah in practice.

Guided Path
1
Trace the etymology and historical naming
Identify that 'Jumu'ah' is derived from 'al-ijtima'' (gathering) and note its pre-Islamic name 'al-Urubah' and its renaming by Ka'b ibn Lu'ayy. This helps understand the communal nature of the prayer.
The name reflects the congregational aspect, which is essential to its ruling.
2
State the ruling and its epistemological basis
The passage explicitly says Jumu'ah is fard 'ayn. Note that denial is kufr because it is established by qat'i (definitive) evidence. Understand the Hanafi position that fard 'ayn is the highest level of obligation.
Qat'i evidence means the ruling is certain, so rejection places one outside the fold.
3
Distinguish Jumu'ah from Zuhr
The commentator emphasizes that Jumu'ah is an independent obligation, stronger than Zuhr, not a substitute. This counters a common misconception that Jumu'ah merely replaces Zuhr.
Independence means Jumu'ah has its own conditions and requirements, not simply a condensed Zuhr.
4
Identify the cross-madhhab difference
While all schools hold Jumu'ah as fard 'ayn, the Hanafi view that denial is kufr is stricter. Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may not consider denial as kufr.
This difference stems from the classification of the evidence as qat'i or zanni.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب صلاة الجمعة في بعض النسخ: باب الجمعة بحذف مضاف لكثرة الاستعمال، وفي بعضها: باب صلاة الجمعة وهو اختيار بعض الشراح، والجمعة مشتقة من الاجتماع؛ لاجتماع الناس أو لما جاء من جمع خلق آدم فيها، أو جمعه مع حواء في الأرض وهي بسكون الميم، والقراء يقرءون بضم الميم، وكان اسمها في الجاهلية العروبة، وقيل: أول من سماها جمعة كعب بن لوي ويسمى يوم المزيد؛ لتزايد الخيرات فيه، أو لتزايد الثواب، وقد يطلق عليه العيد أيضاً وهي فرض عين يكفر جاحدها؛ لثبوتها بالدليل القطعي، وهي فرض مستقل أكد من الظهر، وليست بدلاً عنه والمناسبة بين البابين أن في كليهما سقوط شطر الصلاة أن نسبتها إلى الظهر شطر لا أنها تنصيف الظهر بعينه؛ لأنها فرض ابتداء. (فتح)
Chapter of Friday Prayer: In some copies: 'Chapter of Friday' by omitting the annexed word due to frequent usage, and in some: 'Chapter of the Friday Prayer' and this is the choice of some commentators. Friday (al-Jumu'ah) is derived from gathering (al-ijtima'); because people gather, or because of the gathering of Adam's creation therein, or His gathering (Adam) with Eve on earth. It is pronounced with sukun on the meem, but the reciters read with dammah on the meem. In the Jahiliyyah its name was al-Urubah. It is said the first to name it Jumu'ah was Ka'b ibn Lu'ayy. It is also called the Day of Increase (yawm al-mazid) due to the increase of good deeds or reward. It is also sometimes called 'Eid. It is fard 'ayn; its denier commits kufr because it is established by decisive evidence. It is an independent obligation, stronger than Zuhr, and not a substitute for it. The connection between the two chapters (i.e., the previous and this one) is that in both, half of the prayer is dropped; but its relationship to Zuhr is as a half, not that it is literally half of Zuhr, because it is an original obligation.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is an introductory commentary on the chapter of Friday Prayer. The commentator begins by noting variants in the chapter title: some manuscripts have simply 'Chapter of Friday' while others have 'Chapter of Friday Prayer,' the latter being preferred by some commentators. He then explains the etymology of 'al-Jumu'ah,' deriving it from 'al-ijtima'' (gathering) due to the congregation of people, or the gathering of Adam's creation, or Adam's meeting with Eve on earth. He notes the pronunciation differences (sukun vs. dammah on the meem) and its pre-Islamic name 'al-Urubah.' The first to call it Jumu'ah was Ka'b ibn Lu'ayy. It is also called 'yawm al-mazid' (Day of Increase) because of the multiplication of blessings and rewards. The ruling: Friday prayer is fard 'ayn (individual obligation), and denying it is kufr because it is established by qat'i (definitive) evidence. It is an independent fard, stronger than Zuhr, and not a substitute for it. The commentator explains the relationship between the chapters: in both, half of the prayer is dropped (referring to the shortening of travel prayer and the dropping of Zuhr when Jumu'ah is performed), but Jumu'ah is not half of Zuhr; it is a distinct obligation.

Key Terms
الجمعة (al-jumu'ah): The Friday congregational prayer that is fard 'ayn, derived from gathering.
فرض عين (fard 'ayn): An individual obligation incumbent upon every qualified Muslim.
الدليل القطعي (al-dalil al-qat'i): Definitive evidence that is certain in both transmission and meaning.
